<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Mikania micrantha genome provides insights into the molecular mechanism of rapid growth"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2020 11:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-01-17"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Mikania micrantha is one of the top 100 worst invasive species that can cause serious damage to natural ecosystems and substantial economic losses. Here, we present its 1.79&#8201;Gb chromosome-scale reference genome. Half of the genome is composed of long terminal repeat retrotransposons, 80% of which have been derived from a significant expansion in the past one million years. We identify a whole genome duplication event and recent segmental duplications, which may be responsible for its rapid environmental adaptation. Additionally, we show that M. micrantha achieves higher photosynthetic capacity by CO2 absorption at night to supplement the carbon fixation during the day, as well as enhanced stem photosynthesis efficiency. Furthermore, the metabolites of M. micrantha can increase the availability of nitrogen by enriching the microbes that participate in nitrogen cycling pathways. These findings collectively provide insights into the rapid growth and invasive adaptation. Mikania micrantha is an extremely fast-growing invasive plant species that can cause serious damage to natural ecosystems. Here, the authors assemble its chromosome-scale reference genome and explore possible mechanisms that contribute to its rapid growth."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p><i>Mikania micrantha</i> is one of the top 100 worst invasive species that can cause serious damage to natural ecosystems and substantial economic losses. Here, we present its 1.79 Gb chromosome-scale reference genome. Half of the genome is composed of long terminal repeat retrotransposons, 80% of which have been derived from a significant expansion in the past one million years. We identify a whole genome duplication event and recent segmental duplications, which may be responsible for its rapid environmental adaptation. Additionally, we show that <i>M. micrantha</i> achieves higher photosynthetic capacity by CO<sub>2</sub> absorption at night to supplement the carbon fixation during the day, as well as enhanced stem photosynthesis efficiency. Furthermore, the metabolites of <i>M. micrantha</i> can increase the availability of nitrogen by enriching the microbes that participate in nitrogen cycling pathways. These findings collectively provide insights into the rapid growth and invasive adaptation.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>M<i>ikania micrantha</i> Kunth (“mile-a-minute” weed) is an extremely fast-growing, sprawling, perennial vine belonging to the family Asteraceae and native to tropical America<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Day, M. et al. Biology and impacts of Pacific islands invasive species. 13. Mikania micrantha Kunth (Asteraceae). Pac. Sci. 70, 257–285 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d15635e888">1</a></sup>. The vine is listed as one of the top 100 worst invasive species by the International Union for Conservation of Nature (IUCN)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Lowe S., Browne M., Boudjelas S., De Poorter M. 100 of the World’s Worst Invasive Alien Species, A Selection from the Global Invasive Species Database. (IUCN/SSC Invasive Species Specialist Group, Auckland, 2000)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d15635e892">2</a></sup>. It is problematic in tropical Asia, in parts of Papua New Guinea, on Indian Ocean islands and Pacific Ocean islands and in Florida in the United States<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Zhang, L. Y., Ye, W. H., Cao, H. L. &amp; Feng, H. L. Mikania micrantha H.B.K. in China—an overview. Weed Res. 44, 42–49 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d15635e896">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Manrique, V., Diaz, R., P. Cuda, J. &amp; Overholt, W. Suitability of a new plant invader as a target for Biological control in Florida. Invasive Plant Sci. Manag. 4, 1–10 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d15635e899">4</a></sup>, causing serious damage to the natural ecosystems<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Day, M. et al. Biology and impacts of Pacific islands invasive species. 13. Mikania micrantha Kunth (Asteraceae). Pac. Sci. 70, 257–285 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d15635e903">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Day, M. et al. Mikania micrantha Kunth (Asteraceae) (mile-a-minute): its distribution and physical and socioeconomic impacts in Papua New Guinea. Pac. Sci. 66, 213–223 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d15635e906">5</a></sup>. The invasion of <i>M. micrantha</i> has caused economic losses related to forests and crop production of up to US$4000 ha<sup>−1</sup> <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Zhong, X., Huang, Z., Si, H. &amp; Zan, Q. Analysis of ecological-economic loss caused by weed Mikania micrantha in Neilingding Island, Shenzhen, China. J. Trop. Subtrop. Bot. 12, 167–170 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d15635e916">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Macanawai, A., Day, M., Tumaneng-Diete, T. &amp; Adkins, S. Impact of Mikania micrantha on crop production systems in Viti Levu, Fiji. Pak. J. Weed Sci. Res. 18, 357–365 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d15635e919">7</a></sup> by climbing plants to the canopy, and blocking the sunlight and has led to a loss of genetic and species diversity, a decline in soil and food web stability, and altered nutrient cycling<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Manrique, V., Diaz, R., P. Cuda, J. &amp; Overholt, W. Suitability of a new plant invader as a target for Biological control in Florida. Invasive Plant Sci. Manag. 4, 1–10 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d15635e923">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li, W.-h, Zhang, C.-b, Jiang, H.-b, Xin, G. &amp; Yang, Z.-y Changes in soil microbial community associated with invasion of the exotic weed, Mikania micrantha H.B.K. Plant Soil 281, 309–324 (2006)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d15635e926">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="W-h, Li, C-b, Zhang, G-j, Gao, Q-j, Zan &amp; Z-y, Yang Relationship between Mikania micrantha invasion and soil microbial biomass, respiration and functional diversity. Plant Soil 296, 197–207 (2007)." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d15635e926_1">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kaur, R., Malhotra, S. &amp; Inderjit Effects of invasion of Mikania micrantha on germination of rice seedlings, plant richness, chemical properties and respiration of soil. Biol. Fertil. Soils 48, 481–488 (2012)." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d15635e926_2">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Shen, S. et al. Effects of invasive plant Mikania micrantha on plant community and diversity in farming systems. Asian J. Plant Sci. 14, 27–33 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d15635e929">11</a></sup>.</p><p><i>M. micrantha</i> possesses a variety of biological characteristics associated with successful invasive plant species. It germinates early in the growing season; grows extremely fast (with a maximum mean growth rate of 20 cm day<sup>−1</sup>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Li, M. et al. Evaluation of the controlling methods and strategies for Mikania micrantha H. B. K. Acta Ecol. Sin. 32, 3240–3251 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d15635e940">12</a></sup>; roots from each vine node; has a smothering habit; produces very large numbers of widely dispersed seeds (170,000 m<sup>−2</sup>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Kuo, Y., Chen, T. &amp; Lin, C. Using a consecutive-cutting method and allelopathy to control the invasive vine, Mikania micrantha HBK. Taiwan J. For. Sci. 17, 171–181 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d15635e946">13</a></sup>, which are very small (8.92 × 10<sup>−5</sup> g 1000 grains<sup>−1</sup>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Hu, Y. &amp; But, P. A study on life cycle and response to herbicides of Mikania micrantha. ACTA Sci. Nat. Univ. Sunyatseni 33, 88–95 (1994)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d15635e955">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Yang, Q. et al. An investigation of the effects of environmental factors on the flowering and seed setting of Mikania micrantha HB K (Compositae). J. Trop. Subtrop. Bot. 11, 123–126 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d15635e958">15</a></sup>; and has an ability to survive harsh conditions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Yang, Q. et al. An investigation of the effects of environmental factors on the flowering and seed setting of Mikania micrantha HB K (Compositae). J. Trop. Subtrop. Bot. 11, 123–126 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d15635e962">15</a></sup>. <i>M. micrantha</i> also exhibits a high degree of morphological and physiological plasticity in response to different light environments<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Xiong, D. Morphological and physiological plasticity responding to different light environments of the invasive plant, Mikania micrantha H.B.Kunth. Ecol. Environ. Sci. 19, 1170–1175 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d15635e969">16</a></sup>. In addition, <i>M. micrantha</i> has strong allelopathic effects on other plants and soil microbes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Kaur, R., Malhotra, S. &amp; Inderjit Effects of invasion of Mikania micrantha on germination of rice seedlings, plant richness, chemical properties and respiration of soil. Biol. Fertil. Soils 48, 481–488 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d15635e977">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Chen, B.-M., Peng, S.-L. &amp; Ni, G.-Y. Effects of the invasive plant Mikania micrantha H.B.K. on soil nitrogen availability through allelopathy in South China. Biol. Invasions 11, 1291–1299 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d15635e980">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Wu, A.-P. et al. Differential belowground allelopathic effects of leaf and root of Mikania micrantha. Trees 23, 11–17 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d15635e983">18</a></sup>. Allelopathy is the influence of a plant over a target species, through the release into the environment of compounds that influence the growth and development of biological systems<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Rice E. L. Allelopathy 2nd edn (1983)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d15635e987">19</a></sup>. Various allelochemicals, that are produced by a plant must escape into the environment and subsequently influence the growth and development of other plants, such as <i>Mikania</i> sesquiterpene lactones (STLs)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Shao, H., Peng, S., Wei, X., Zhang, D. &amp; Zhang, C. Potential allelochemicals from an invasive weed Mikania micrantha H.B.K. J. Chem. Ecol. 31, 1657–1668 (2005)." href="#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d15635e994">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Huang, H. J., Ye, W. H., Wei, X. Y. &amp; Zhang, C. X. Allelopathic potential of sesquiterpene lactones and phenolic constituents from Mikania micrantha H. B. K. Biochem Syst. Ecol. 36, 867–871 (2008)." href="#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d15635e994_1">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Piyasena, K. G. N. P. &amp; Dharmaratne, H. R. W. Allelopathic activity studies of Mikania scandens. Nat. Prod. Res. 27, 76–79 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d15635e997">22</a></sup> and phenolic compounds<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Xu, Q. L., Xie, H. H., Xiao, H. L. &amp; Wei, X. Y. Phenolic constituents from the roots of Mikania micrantha and their allelopathic effects. J. Agr. Food Chem. 61, 7309–7314 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d15635e1001">23</a></sup> have been isolated from different <i>M. micrantha</i> plant tissues. However, the molecular mechanism underlying the fast growth of <i>M. micrantha</i> and the biosynthetic pathways of its characteristic allelochemicals are not yet clear.</p><p>Asteraceae is not only one of the largest, most valuable plant families, but also contains the largest number of invasive alien species worldwide. Genomes of some important species of Asteraceae, including lettuce<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Reyes-Chin-Wo, S. et al. Genome assembly with in vitro proximity ligation data and whole-genome triplication in lettuce. Nat. Commun. 8, 14953 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d15635e1015">24</a></sup>, sunflower<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Badouin, H. et al. The sunflower genome provides insights into oil metabolism, flowering and Asterid evolution. Nature 546, 148–152 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d15635e1019">25</a></sup>, artichoke<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Scaglione, D. et al. The genome sequence of the outbreeding globe artichoke constructed de novo incorporating a phase-aware low-pass sequencing strategy of F1 progeny. Sci. Rep. 6, 19427 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d15635e1023">26</a></sup>, and horseweed<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Peng, Y. et al. De novo genome assembly of the economically important weed horseweed using integrated data from multiple sequencing platforms. Plant Physiol. 166, 1241–1254 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d15635e1027">27</a></sup>, have been sequenced, deepening our understanding of Asteraceae evolution and promoting biological studies of plants in Asteraceae. Although some studies have examined the biological characteristics of <i>M. micrantha</i>, genomic and molecular data are scarce, which limits in-depth studies of this invasive species. Here, we generate a reference genome of <i>M. micrantha</i> and perform analyses of transcriptomic, metabolomics, and metagenomic data to explore possible reasons causing its rapid growth.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Chromosome-level genome assembly and recent LTR-RT expansion</h3><p>We generated 228 Gb (122 × coverage) of single molecule real-time (SMRT) sequences on the PacBio RS II and Sequel platform (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">1</a>); these sequences were used to assemble a haploid reference genome by Canu-1.6, followed by filtering of the extra heterozygous fragments (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">1</a> and Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">1</a>). The assembled genome includes 4414 contigs with a total length of 1.79 Gb, a contig N50 of 1352 kb, and a contig N90 of 180 Kb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">2</a>). Based on the distribution of k-mer frequencies, the estimated genome size of <i>M. micrantha</i> is 1.86 Gb (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">2</a>); thus, 96% of the <i>M. micrantha</i> genome has been successfully assembled. With Hi-C technology, 1.61 Gb (90.3%) of contigs was anchored and oriented into the 19 linkage groups (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig1">1a</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">2</a>), with the longest being 124 Mb and the shortest being 47 Mb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Tab1">1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics of the <i>Mikaina micrantha</i> genome and gene prediction.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Landscape of the Mikania micrantha genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Landscape of the <i>Mikania micrantha</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-13926-4/MediaObjects/41467_2019_13926_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-13926-4/MediaObjects/41467_2019_13926_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="396" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b> Integration of genomic and expression data. (a) Distribution of GC content; (b) distribution of the Gypsy family of long terminal repeats retrotransposons (LTR-RTs); (c) distribution of the Copia family of LTR-RTs; (d) distribution of coding genes; (e–h) expression of organ-specific genes (from outside to inside tracks: root, stem, leaf and flower). <b>b</b> Comparison of repetitive sequences in asterids, including <i>Mikania micrantha</i>, sunflower (<i>Helianthus annuus</i>), artichoke thistle (<i>Cynara cardunculus</i>), <i>Artemisia annua</i>, chrysanthemum (<i>Dendranthema morifolium</i>), lettuce (<i>Lactuca sativa</i>), and coffee (<i>Coffea canephora</i>). <b>c</b> The insertion time distribution of intact LTRs in the <i>M. micrantha</i> genome. Mya indicates million years ago.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The protein-coding genes in the reference genome were predicted by the EVidenceModeler pipeline (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">3</a>). In total, 46,351 gene models were predicted in the assembled genome as the reference gene set, with coding regions spanning ~57.6 Mb (3.2%) of the genome (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">4</a>). In the evaluation of completeness, 91% of eukaryote core genes from OrthoDB (<a href="http://www.orthodb.org">http://www.orthodb.org</a>) were identified as complete in the reference gene set by BUSCO (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Tab1">1</a>), comparable to the number for other published genomes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">3</a>). A total of 42,804 (92%) coding proteins were annotated by functional databases (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">5</a>), including eggNOG, KEGG, Interpro, and uniprot. In addition, we identified 3633 long noncoding RNA (lncRNAs) classified into three subclasses: 2489 intergenic lncRNAs (lincRNAs), 551 intronic lncRNAs (ilncRNAs), and 593 antisense lncRNAs (NAT-lncRNAs) (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">4</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">6</a>).</p><p>The high coverage and continuity of the <i>M. micrantha</i> genome enabled a comprehensive analysis of transposable elements (TEs). In total, more than three quarters of the <i>M. micrantha</i> genome consists of TEs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig1">1b</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">6</a>), 60% of which are long terminal repeat retrotransposons (LTR-RTs). Notably, the most abundant LTR-RTs family present in <i>M. micrantha</i> was <i>Gypsy</i>, accounting for 70.1% of all LTR elements, followed by <i>Copia</i> (26.1%) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">7</a>). LTR-RTs are the most abundant DNA components in all investigated plant species and are largely responsible for plant genome size variation. Interestingly, over 80% of the LTR-RT lineages in <i>M. micrantha</i> are very young, exhibiting minimal sequence divergence compared to those of other plant species, owing to significant expansion in the past 1 million years (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig1">1c</a>), which is consistent with previous reports in sunflower<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Badouin, H. et al. The sunflower genome provides insights into oil metabolism, flowering and Asterid evolution. Nature 546, 148–152 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d15635e1311">25</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome-wide duplication and subsequent fragment duplication</h3><p>To assess the paleohistory of the asterids, we performed a comparative genomic investigation of <i>M. micrantha</i> with sunflower (<i>Helianthus annuus</i>), lettuce (<i>Lactuca sativa</i>), artichoke thistle (<i>Cynara cardunculus</i>), chrysanthemum (<i>Dendranthema morifolium</i>), <i>Artemisia annua</i> and coffee (<i>Coffea canephora</i>) with grape (<i>Vitis vinifera</i>) as an outgroup, showing that the <i>M. micrantha</i> diverged from grape ~109 million years ago (Mya), from artichoke ~51 Mya, and from lettuce ~43 Mya. Notably, <i>M. micrantha</i> and sunflower were diverged ~31 Mya (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig2">2a</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Genome evolution of Mikania micrantha."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Genome evolution of <i>Mikania micrantha</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-13926-4/MediaObjects/41467_2019_13926_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-13926-4/MediaObjects/41467_2019_13926_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="578" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b> The paleohistory of the asterids, including <i>M. micrantha</i>, sunflower, lettuce, artichoke, chrysanthemum, <i>Artemisia annua</i>, and coffee, with grape as an outgroup. A whole-genome duplication event (WGD-2) occurring before <i>M. micrantha-</i>sunflower divergence (approximately 32–36 Mya), and the shared ancestral whole genome triplications WGT-γ (approximately 122–164 Mya) and WGT-1 (approximately 53–62 Mya). Circles indicate the ages of WGT-1 (blue), WGD-2 (gray) and SD-3 (red). <b>b</b> <i>K</i><sub><i>s</i></sub> distribution of syntenic orthologues from <i>M. micrantha</i>, sunflower and artichoke. The <i>y</i>-axis shows the ratio of gene pairs in the syntenic block. The polyploidization (WGT-1, WGD-2, and SD-3) and speciation (<i>M. micrantha</i>-sunflower) events are referenced on the peaks. <b>c</b> Dot plots of syntenic orthologues in two chromosomes between <i>M. micrantha</i> and sunflower. <b>d</b> The functional enrichment of WGT-1, WGD-2, and SD-3 duplicated genes in <i>M. micrantha</i> by Gene Ontology (GO) classification. The significantly enriched GO terms for the SD-3 and WGT-1/WGT2 duplicated genes are marked by red and blue, respectively.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To study the conservation of genomic structure, we identified the interspecies syntenic genes between <i>M. micrantha</i> and sunflower, artichoke, and grape, and intraspecies syntenic genes within the <i>M. micrantha</i>, sunflower, lettuce, artichoke, and coffee genomes, then calculated the <i>K</i><sub><i>s</i></sub> values of the syntenic fragments for orthologous pairs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig2">2b, c</a> and Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">8</a>). These results, together with the results of previous reports<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Badouin, H. et al. The sunflower genome provides insights into oil metabolism, flowering and Asterid evolution. Nature 546, 148–152 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d15635e1439">25</a></sup>, showed that the <i>M. micrantha</i> genome has a complex paleo-polyploidy history, including the ancient whole-genome triplication WGT-γ (approximately 122–164 Mya) in dicotyledons, the whole-genome triplication WGT-1 (approximately 53–62 Mya) shared with asterids II, and the whole-genome duplication WGD-2 (approximately 32–36 Mya) shared with sunflower. In contrast to the genomes of other species in Asteraceae, the <i>M. micrantha</i> genome exhibits a large number of recent segmental duplications. With <i>K</i><sub><i>s</i></sub> values lower than 0.2, we identified 127 syntenic blocks ranging from 46 to 8955 kb in length and containing 23% of all syntenic genes, indicating that the <i>M. micrantha</i> genome experienced a recent explosion of lineage-specific segmental duplications (SD-3, less than 1 Mya; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig2">2b</a>).</p><p>Large-scale genome duplications produce abundant duplicated genes, which are important for the diversity of gene functions and adaptation to changing environments. The WGT-1, WGD-2, and SD-3 duplicated genes were significantly enriched in the functions of catalytic and hydrolase activity, nitrogen compound metabolism, transcription factor, response to stimulus and chemical (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig2">2d</a> and Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">10</a>). It demonstrated that extensive gene fractionation has occurred during the evolutionary history of the <i>M. micrantha</i> genome, which retained the genes that are essential for survival and lost other redundant genes. Next, gene families related to fast growth were analyzed. A total of 189 photosynthesis related genes were identified in the <i>M. micrantha</i> genome, and the number of genes related to photoreaction was significantly higher than that in other Asteraceae species (<i>p</i> &lt; 0.01, Z-test; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">11</a>). The large-scale duplication prompted photosynthesis gene expansion, with 5% of these genes produced after the WGT-1 and WGD-2 events and 21% of these genes produced after recent SD-3 events. In addition, auxin signaling and jasmonic acid (JA) biosynthesis genes were significantly expanded in the <i>M. micrantha</i> genome, compared with the genomes of other Asteraceae species (<i>p</i> &lt; 0.01, Z-test; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">12a, b</a>). The duplicated genes, which were enriched in categories of genes encoding interacting products, photosynthesis, hormone, and response to stress, play an important role in the environmental adaptability of plants.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">High photosynthesis of the leaves benefits rapid growth</h3><p>In this study, the diurnal carbon isotope (δ<sup>13</sup>C) changes in <i>M. micrantha</i> leaves were not significantly different between day and night based on analysis of the carbon isotope ratio (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">13</a>), indicating that <i>M. micrantha</i> has a typical C<sub>3</sub> carbon assimilation type, consistent with the findings of previous reports<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Day, M. et al. Biology and impacts of Pacific islands invasive species. 13. Mikania micrantha Kunth (Asteraceae). Pac. Sci. 70, 257–285 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d15635e1518">1</a></sup>. However, the net photosynthetic rate of leaves was higher than that in other C<sub>3</sub> plants and similar to that in C<sub>4</sub> plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Day, M. et al. Biology and impacts of Pacific islands invasive species. 13. Mikania micrantha Kunth (Asteraceae). Pac. Sci. 70, 257–285 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d15635e1526">1</a></sup>. Previous studies revealed that all plants contain the genes necessary for Crassulacean acid metabolism (CAM) photosynthesis, but the genes regulating the CAM pathway are often silent or inactivated in non-CAM plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Ming, R. et al. The pineapple genome and the evolution of CAM photosynthesis. Nat. Genet. 47, 1435 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d15635e1530">28</a></sup>. However, the expression of genes related to CAM photosynthesis is activated in response to changes in the external environment or in certain growth stages<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Gawronska, K., Romanowska, E., Miszalski, Z. &amp; Niewiadomska, E. Limitation of C3-CAM shift in the common ice plant under high irradiance. J. Plant Physiol. 170, 129–135 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d15635e1534">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Adams, P. et al. Growth and development of Mesembryanthemum crystallinum (Aizoaceae). New Phytol. 138, 171–190 (1998)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d15635e1537">30</a></sup>. To adapt to environmental changes, a few plants have evolved to use both the C<sub>3</sub> and CAM photosynthesis pathways, with interconversion between growth stages<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Winter, K. &amp; Holtum, J. The effects of salinity, crassulacean acid metabolism and plant age on the carbon isotope composition of Mesembryanthemum crystallinum L., a halophytic C3-CAM species. Planta 222, 201–209 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d15635e1544">31</a></sup>.</p><p>Here, we identified genes related to the CAM pathway based on homology to Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (KEGG) pathways. The <i>M. micrantha</i> genome contains 51 candidate genes involved in the carbon fixation module of CAM, including carbonic anhydrase (CA), phosphoenolpyruvate carboxylase (PEPC), phosphoenolpyruvate carboxykinase (PEPCK), malic enzyme (ME), malate dehydrogenase (MDH), and pyruvate orthophosphate dikinase (PPDK) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig3">3a</a>). The gene copy number of the CAM pathway in <i>M. micrantha</i> was 1.7 and 1.4 times greater than that in pineapple (a typical CAM plant) and sunflower (a C<sub>3</sub> plant), respectively. In addition, the activity of PEPC in the leaves of <i>M. micrantha</i> (2.8 μmol mg<sup>−1</sup> protein) was higher than that in pineapple leaves (2.0 μmol mg<sup>−1</sup> protein).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="The special photosynthetic system in Mikania micrantha."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: The special photosynthetic system in <i>Mikania micrantha.</i></b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-13926-4/MediaObjects/41467_2019_13926_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-13926-4/MediaObjects/41467_2019_13926_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="408" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b> The number of putative carbon fixation genes in <i>M. micrantha</i>, pineapple and sunflower. The gene number of pineapple was obtained from the literature<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Ming, R. et al. The pineapple genome and the evolution of CAM photosynthesis. Nat. Genet. 47, 1435 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d15635e1585">28</a></sup>; carbonic anhydrase (CA), phosphoenolpyruvate carboxylase (PEPC), phosphoenolpyruvate carboxykinase (PEPCK), malic enzyme (ME), malate dehydrogenase (MDH), and pyruvate orthophosphate dikinase (PPDK). <b>b</b> Expression pattern of CAM pathway key genes in <i>M. micrantha</i> across the diurnal expression data. The R-package (pheatmap)-transformed transcripts per kilobase million (TMP) expression profiles are shown. The gene names of <i>M. micrantha</i> are shown on the right. The gene expression of each replicate sample in two time points (9 A.M. and 9 P.M.) presented in the heatmap. <b>c</b> The diurnal variation pattern of key enzyme activities and malic acid content in <i>M. micrantha</i> leaves at 9:00 A.M. (day) and 9:00 P.M. (night). The <i>y</i>-axis on the left shows the activity of PEPC and NADP-linked ME. Statistical significance (<i>n</i> = 3) determined using the two-sided Student’s <i>t</i> test. On each box plot, the central mark indicates the median, the bottom and top edges of the box indicate the interquartile range (IQR) and the whiskers represent the maximum and minimum data points. <b>d</b> Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) of stomata in <i>M. micrantha</i> leaves during the day and at night. SEM images of the samples were taken using a scanning electron microscope (Q25, FEI, USA). <b>e</b> Schematic of the CAM and C<sub>3</sub> photosynthesis proposed pathways of <i>M. micrantha</i>. The enzymes in the CAM pathway of <i>M. micrantha</i> are shown in red. During the day, carbon dioxide is absorbed directly by the leaves through the C<sub>3</sub> pathway. At night, carbon dioxide is fixed nocturnally by PEPC and stored transiently as malic acid in large vacuoles. Furthermore, carbon dioxide is released from organic acids through NADP-linked ME and used directly in the Calvin cycle in the daytime. The source data underlying Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig3">3</a>b–d are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To investigate the enzyme activity patterns of the CAM pathway in day and night, we collected RNA-seq samples at 9:00 A.M. (day) and 9:00 P.M. (night) from leaf tissues of potted <i>M. micrantha</i> (see Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">2</a>). On the basis of contrasting expression patterns in the leaf tissues between day and night, we identified the diurnal variation of <i>M. micrantha</i> genes involved in carbon fixation (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig3">3b</a>). The eight key genes of CAM pathway (encoding three copies of CA, one copy of PEPC and two copies of NADP-linked ME) had a diurnal expression pattern in the leaf tissue (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig3">3b</a>). In addition, the enzyme activity of <i>M. micrantha</i> PEPC in the leaf tissue was higher in the night than that during the day (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig3">3c</a>). Carbon dioxide was fixed nocturnally by PEPC and stored transiently as malic acid in large vacuoles; consequently the malic acid and citric acid content in the evening was nearly 10 times and two times as high as that in the daytime, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig3">3c</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">14a</a>). The stored malic acid was decarboxylated, and the carbon dioxide was released by NADP-linked ME during the daytime; thus, the activity of NADP-linked ME showed a pattern in which the activity in the daytime was significantly higher than that at night (<i>p</i> = 0.027, two-sided Student’s <i>t</i> test, Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig3">3c</a>).</p><p>In addition, the stomata of C<sub>3</sub> and C<sub>4</sub> plants open to absorb carbon dioxide during the day, while they close at night, except for in CAM plants, whose stomata exhibit a unique pattern of diurnal variation. Surprisingly, the stomata of <i>M. micrantha</i> leaves were not only always open during the day (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig3">3d</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">14b, c</a>), but also partly open at night (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig3">3d</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">14b, c</a>), implying that the CAM pathway was involved in the carbon dioxide fixation. Together, these results indicate that one of the reasons for the higher photosynthetic capacity of <i>M. micrantha</i> leaves is that CO<sub>2</sub> may be absorbed at night by photosynthetic pathway similar to CAM and stored in vacuoles as organic acids to supplement the carbon fixation capacity during the day (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig3">3e</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">High photosynthesis of the stem enhances rapid growth</h3><p>In most plants, the leaves are the major photosynthetic organs; however, some studies have reported that many nonleaf green organs or tissues also have photosynthetic activity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Aschan, G. &amp; Pfanz, H. Non-foliar photosynthesis—a strategy of additional carbon acquisition. Flora 198, 81–97 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d15635e1720">32</a></sup>. To investigate the photosynthetic ability of stems in <i>M. micrantha</i>, we measured their net photosynthetic rate (Pn), respiration and gross photosynthetic rate and compared these values with those of five other species. The Pn of the <i>M. micrantha</i> stem was approximately 1.5 μmol CO<sub>2</sub> m<sup>−2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, and was significantly higher than that in <i>Paederia scandens</i>, <i>Pueraria lobata</i>, <i>Stephania longa</i>, <i>Merremia hederacea</i>, and <i>Ipomoea cairica</i> (<i>p</i> &lt; 0.05, Duncan-test; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig4">4a</a>), which was close to or less than 0 μmol CO<sub>2</sub> m<sup>−2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>. The gross photosynthetic rate of the <i>M. micrantha</i> stem showed a similar pattern (<i>p</i> &lt; 0.05, Duncan-test; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">15</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Identification of stem photosynthesis in Mikania micrantha."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Identification of stem photosynthesis in <i>Mikania micrantha</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-13926-4/MediaObjects/41467_2019_13926_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-13926-4/MediaObjects/41467_2019_13926_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="705" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b> Comparison of the net photosynthetic rate of <i>M. micrantha</i> to that of five neighboring species, namely, <i>Paederia scandens</i>, <i>Pueraria lobata</i>, <i>Stephania longa</i>, <i>Merremia hederacea</i>, and <i>Ipomoea cairica</i>. Statistical significance (<i>n</i> = 3) determined using one-way ANOVA with Duncan’s multiple comparison test. <b>b</b> The change in chlorophyll content in the stem under defoliation conditions compared to that in the control. Statistical significance (<i>n</i> = 3) determined using the two-sided Student’s <i>t</i> test. On each box plot, the central mark indicates the median, the bottom and top edges of the box indicate the interquartile range (IQR) and the whiskers represent the maximum and minimum data points. <b>c</b> KEGG pathway enrichment of upregulated genes. The significant enrichment of KEGG pathways related to photosynthesis, carbon fixation and hormone signal transduction are marked in red. <b>d</b> Comparison of key genes related to the Calvin cycle in the <i>M. micrantha</i> stem between defoliation conditions and the control. The significantly upregulated genes are marked in red. <b>e</b> Expression pattern of differentially expressed genes involved in auxin, cytokinin, gibberellin, and chlorophyll biosynthesis and plant hormone signal transduction. The gene names of <i>M. micrantha</i> are shown on the right. Error bars indicate average value ± s.e.m. of indicated replicates. The source data underlying Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig4">4</a>a, b, e are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To confirm that the photosynthesis of the <i>M. micrantha</i> stem could maintain plant growth, we conducted a defoliation experiment with both <i>M. micrantha</i> and its neighboring species <i>P. scandens</i> (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">3</a>). All the <i>M. micrantha</i> samples grew normally without leaves for 30 days, but only 10% of the <i>P. scandens</i> samples survived (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">16</a>). In addition, the outer epidermis of the stem of <i>M. micrantha</i> gradually changed from red to green in the defoliation treatment (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">17a</a>). The major reason for this change was a significant reduction in the anthocyanin content (<i>p</i> = 0.001, two-sided Student’s <i>t</i> test), which was more than ten times lower than that in the stems of the undefoliated samples (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">17b</a>), indicating that the stem of <i>M. micrantha</i> can compensate for its photosynthetic capacity by reducing anthocyanin accumulation and absorbing more external light energy. In addition, the stem of <i>M. micrantha</i> had the same photosynthetic components as the leaves, such as stomata and chlorophyll. The chlorophyll content in the stem of <i>M. micrantha</i> increased significantly in the defoliation treatment, but no significant changes occurred in <i>P. scandens</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig4">4b</a>). Moreover, the number of grana lamellae increased, and the synthesized starch grains were also significantly larger than those in the control stems, which were similar to the patterns in the leaves (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">18</a>). In addition, we found that the plant hormone (auxin, gibberellin, and cytokinin) contents in the <i>M. micrantha</i> stem after defoliation were significantly higher than that in the control group, and similar to that in the leaves (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">19a–c</a>).</p><p>To investigate the gene expression patterns of the photosynthesis pathway genes in the stem of <i>M. micrantha</i>, we collected RNA-seq samples from defoliated and control stems. The upregulated genes were significantly enriched in the photosynthesis (<i>p</i> = 1.6 × 10<sup>−4</sup>, Deseq2), carbon fixation (<i>p</i> = 2.7 × 10<sup>−9</sup>, Deseq2), and plant hormone signal transduction pathways (<i>p</i> = 5.4 × 10<sup>−5</sup>, Deseq2; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig4">4c</a>). The anthocyanin contents were markedly reduced in the defoliation treatment because the expression of anthocyanin synthesis genes in this treatment were significantly downregulated compared with that in the control (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">17c, d</a>). Six key genes in the Calvin cycle of the photosynthesis pathway showed significant upregulation in the defoliation treatment (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig4">4d</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">20</a>). Ribulose-1,5-bisphosphate carboxylase/oxygenase (Rubisco) is an enzyme involved in the first major step of carbon fixation and plays an important role in the process of atmospheric carbon dioxide conversion by plants. The gene expression of five Rubisco copies was four times higher than that in the control group (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">20</a>), suggesting that the stem maintains the plant growth by regulating the expression of Rubisco in the absence of leaves. Furthermore, the chlorophyll and plant hormone signal transduction and synthesis pathways displayed similar gene expression patterns, in which the genes were significantly upregulated in the treatment group (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig4">4e</a>). This evidence indicated that under the defoliation stress, the <i>M. micrantha</i> gained enough energy for rapid stem growth and development by improving its photosynthetic rate and increasing its chlorophyll and hormone contents.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Genetic pathway of <i>Mikania</i> STLs</h3><p>STLs, which are mainly derived from the precursor germacrene A acid (GAA), are known for their allelopathic effects and are characteristic of the family Asteraceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Rice E. L. Allelopathy 2nd edn (1983)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d15635e1963">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Picman, A. K. Biological activities of sesquiterpene lactones. Biochem. Syst. Ecol. 14, 255–281 (1986)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d15635e1966">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Bais, H. P., Ramarao, V., Simon, G., Callaway, R. M. &amp; Vivanco, J. M. Allelopathy and exotic plant invasion: from molecules and genes to species interactions. Science 301, 1377–1380 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d15635e1969">34</a></sup>. Several enzymes acting downstream of GAA have been elucidated recently, such as HaG8H, LsCOS, and Ih8H, that lead to the formation of different STLs with distinctive stereochemistry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ikezawa, N. et al. Lettuce costunolide synthase (CYP71BL2) and its homolog (CYP71BL1) from sunflower catalyze distinct regio- and stereoselective hydroxylations in sesquiterpene lactone metabolism. J. Biol. Chem. 286, 21601–21611 (2011)." href="#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d15635e1973">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Frey, M., Schmauder, K., Pateraki, I. &amp; Spring, O. Biosyinthesis of Eupatolide—a metabolic route for sesquiterpene lactone formation involving the P450 enzyme CYP71DD6. Acs Chem. Biol. 13, 1536–1543 (2018)." href="#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d15635e1973_1">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Gou, J. B. et al. Discovery of a non-stereoselective cytochrome P450 catalyzing either 8 alpha- or 8 beta-hydroxylation of germacrene A acid from the Chinese medicinal plant, Inula hupehensis. Plant J. 93, 92–106 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d15635e1976">37</a></sup>. However, the biosynthetic pathways of STLs in <i>Mikania</i> remain unknown. Lactone ring formation in STLs is dependent upon the (C6 or C8) position and stereoselective hydroxylations of GAA, which can result in STLs with four unique configurations (12, 6α-, 12, 6β-, 12, 8α-, and 12, 8β-olides derivatives of GAA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Gou, J. B. et al. Discovery of a non-stereoselective cytochrome P450 catalyzing either 8 alpha- or 8 beta-hydroxylation of germacrene A acid from the Chinese medicinal plant, Inula hupehensis. Plant J. 93, 92–106 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d15635e1983">37</a></sup>. Ih8H homolog is important enzyme convert to 7, 8-trans lactone from GAA. As all the detected STLs in this study had a 7, 8-trans lactone, we were investigating for a homolog of Ih8H, an enzyme capable of synthesizing 7, 8-trans inunolide, the putative precursor of these <i>M. micrantha</i> STLs.</p><p>We reconstructed a genome-scale co-expression network for <i>M. micrantha</i> and focused on the metabolic pathways involved in inunolide synthesis, yielding a total of 38 genes in this pathway (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig5">5a</a>). Five genes previously reported to be involved in GAA synthesis, namely, germacrene A oxidases (GAOs), germacrene A synthase (GAS), and GAA 8-hydroxylase (Ih8H), which have high identity with the genes in <i>H. annuus</i>, <i>A. annua</i>, <i>L. sativa</i>, and <i>Inula hupehensis</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">8</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">21</a>), were identified in the inunolide synthesis pathway and showed the high expression in the leaf and flower (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">22</a>). Inunolide is a key intermediate of STL products, which can be identified and detected in fresh leaves by ultra-performance liquid chromatography (UPLC)-quadrupole time-of-flight-mass spectrometry (MS) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">23</a>). In addition, we have developed a liquid chromatograph MS (LC–MS) method to determine the content of inunolide (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig5">5b</a>), which displayed the same pattern as the expression of the five genes in different tissues (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">22</a>). This suggests that inunolide is likely as intermediate to the STLs metabolites in <i>Mikania</i>. Thus, the proposed pathway of STLs in <i>M. micrantha</i> was displayed in green arrows (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig5">5c</a>). According to the co-expression network, the candidate genes of STLs biosynthesis were detected, which may regulate the pathway producing STLs in <i>M. micrantha</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig5">5a</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">9</a>). Based on the previous research<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Nelson, D. &amp; Werck-Reichhart, D. A P450-centric view of plant evolution. Plant J. 66, 194–211 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2050">38</a></sup>, the candidate genes of STLs biosynthesis were assigned to CYP71 subfamily of P450 enzyme. Quantitative analysis of five available STLs (anhydroscandenolide, deoxymikanolide, dihydromikanolide, scandenolide, and 3-epi-dihydroscandenolide) showed a significant higher STLs content in the leaf and flower (<i>p</i> &lt; 0.05, Duncan-test), but almost no STLs were detected in stem and root (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig5">5d and Supplementary Fig. 27a</a>). Microscopic studies revealed the high density of glandular trichomes occurred in flowers (petal) followed by leaves, less glandular trichomes were observed in stem surface (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">24</a>). Most of STLs were extracted from glandular trichomes with the chloroform dipping extraction method (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">25</a>). The content of five STLs in flowers and leaves trichomes present a higher abundance, but less in the stem trichomes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">26</a>), which further proved the STLs were derived from <i>Mikania</i> trichomes. These results are consistent with those of gene expression, which supported the proposed pathway of STLs in <i>M. micrantha</i>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Investigation of sesquiterpene lactones and proposed biosynthetic pathway."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Investigation of sesquiterpene lactones and proposed biosynthetic pathway.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-13926-4/MediaObjects/41467_2019_13926_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-13926-4/MediaObjects/41467_2019_13926_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="877" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>a</b> Coexpression network of the sesquiterpene lactones (STLs) biosynthesis pathway elements with a correlation coefficient (&gt;0.7). The genes (GAO germacrene A oxidase, GAS germacrene A synthase, Ih8H germacrene A acid 8-hydroxylae), that were reported in previous studies, are colored in orange. The candidate genes involved in STLs biosynthesis from inunolide are colored in red. <b>b</b> The relative inunolide content in different fresh plant tissues (<i>n</i> = 3). L, F, S, and R represent the leaf, flower, stem, and root, respectively. <b>c</b> The hypothesized biosynthesis pathway of STLs from germacrene A acid. Green arrows indicate the proposed metabolic pathways of <i>Mikania micrantha</i> STLs. The candidate genes of the biosynthesis pathway of <i>M. micrantha</i> STLs are indicated by blue boxes. The black arrows indicate the biosynthetic pathway of STLs in sunflower from a previous report<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Frey, M., Schmauder, K., Pateraki, I. &amp; Spring, O. Biosyinthesis of Eupatolide—a metabolic route for sesquiterpene lactone formation involving the P450 enzyme CYP71DD6. Acs Chem. Biol. 13, 1536–1543 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2101">36</a></sup>. FPP farnesyl pyrophosphate, GAA germacrene A acid. <b>d</b> The amount of five STLs in fresh tissues (<i>n</i> = 3). Anh anhydroscandenolide, Deo deoxymikanolide, Dih dihydromikanolide, Sca scandenolide, 3-epi 3-epi-dihydroscandenolide. <b>e</b> Pot experiments to simulate the release of <i>M. micrantha</i> STLs into soil. Five treatments: Add leaves, dried <i>M. micrantha</i> leaves were added to the soil surface in each pot to measure STLs in the soil, mainly coming from the fallen leaves. Add flowers, dried flowers were added to the soil surface in each pot to measure STLs mainly from fallen flowers. Nothing added, nothing was added to determine STLs in the soil from root exudates. Leaching, the plants were watered to wet the growing leaves and thus determine the STLs that were mainly derived from leaching. Natural growth, this was used as the control experiment. <i>n</i> = 4 biologically independent samples. <b>d</b>, <b>e</b> Duncan-test (<i>p</i> &lt; 0.05) was used to determine statistical significance. Different letters above the error bars indicate significant differences among means for the different parts of the plant or treatments. Error bars indicate average value ± s.d. of indicated replicates. The source data underlying Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig5">5</a>b, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig5">d</a>, e are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Five STLs, including the dominant constituent dihydromikanolide and scandenolide, were identified in invaded soils collected from different regions (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">27b</a>). The STLs content in soil differed among the collection regions, which may have been related to community age, i.e., invasion history, or other ecological factors in the field. To better understand how the STLs are released into surrounding soil, a pot experiment was conducted to determine where the STLs in soil come from (litter fall from the aerial part, root exudates or leaching) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig5">5e</a> and Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">4</a>). Five STLs were also detected in the soil after 15 days of treatment, and the STL levels in the treatments with added <i>M. micrantha</i> leaves and added flowers were higher than those in the treatments with root exudates (nothing added) and leaching (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig5">5e</a>). Five STLs that were almost undetectable in the root by LC–MS analysis accumulated in the leaves and flowers, which suggested that these five STLs were released into the environment by litter fall and rain, not by root exudates. In addition, the five STLs were detected in soil invaded by <i>M. micrantha</i>, providing reliable evidence that STLs are typical allelochemicals of the “mile-a-minute” weed. The proposed STLs pathway based on metabolic and genetic data will promote further investigation of the function of allelochemicals in <i>M. micrantha</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Reinforced microbial function promotes nutrient utilization</h3><p>To investigate the potential effects of <i>M. micrantha</i> on soil nutrient cycling, we conducted a pot experiment with <i>M. micrantha</i> and its two neighboring native species, namely, <i>Polygonum chinense</i> and <i>Paederia scandens</i> (see Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">5</a>). The total nitrogen in <i>M. micrantha</i> soil significantly decreased, compared to that in the soil of its two neighboring species and the control which no plants were planted (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig6">6a</a>). Conversely, the available nitrogen, particularly ammonium nitrogen, in <i>M. micrantha</i> soil increased significantly more than that in the soil of its two neighboring native species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig6">6a</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">10</a>). In addition, the variation in phosphorus and potassium content in soil showed a similar trend (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">10</a>). Furthermore, we determined the contents of nitrogen, phosphorus and potassium contents in different plant tissues. The accumulation of the three elements in the different tissues of <i>M. micrantha</i> was significantly higher than that in its two neighbors (<i>P. chinense</i> and <i>P. scandens</i>) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">28</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Effect of Mikania micrantha metabolites on soil nitrogen cycling."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6: Effect of <i>Mikania micrantha</i> metabolites on soil nitrogen cycling.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-13926-4/MediaObjects/41467_2019_13926_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-13926-4/MediaObjects/41467_2019_13926_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="627" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>STLs: sesquiterpene lactones. <b>a</b> Soil nitrogen content in the pot experiment of <i>M. micrantha</i> and its two neighboring native species. <i>n</i> = 6 biologically independent soil samples. We used Duncan’s multiple range test (<i>p</i> &lt; 0.05) method. Data with different letters in the same column are significantly different. Control indicates that no plants were planted. Error bars indicate average value ± s.d. of indicated replicates. <b>b</b> Box plots of the relative abundance of genes involved in nitrogen metabolism pathway.
