Adjusted p value was calculated using Dunn’s test. <i>n</i> = 5 biologically independent soil samples. <b>c</b> The relative abundance of key genes of soil microorganisms involved in assimilatory nitrogen reduction (<i>nasA</i>), dissimilatory nitrogen reduction (<i>napA</i>), ammonification (<i>ureC</i>), and denitrification (<i>norB</i>) between <i>M. micrantha</i> and its two neighboring species (<i>Polygonum chinense</i> and <i>Paederia scandens</i>) as well as in the blank control. Adjusted <i>p</i> value was calculated using Dunn’s test. <i>n</i> = 5 biologically independent soil samples. On each box plot, the central mark indicates the median, the bottom and top edges of the box indicate the interquartile range (IQR) and the whiskers represent the maximum and minimum data points. <b>d</b> A schematic of nitrogen cycling in the <i>M. micrantha</i> soil. Red and green indicate that the abundance of the gene in <i>M. micrantha</i> soil was upregulated and downregulated compared to that in the soil of the two neighboring species (<i>P. chinense</i> and <i>P. scandens</i>), respectively. Black, orange, red, blue, gray, and purple arrows indicate the denitrification, nitrification, nitrogen fixation, dissimilatory nitrogen reduction, assimilatory nitrogen reduction, and ammonification, respectively. The source data underlying Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig6">6</a>a–c are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Soil metagenomics provides a cultivation-independent assessment of the largely untapped genetic reservoir of soil microbial communities, which has already led to the identification of functional biomolecules<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Daniel, R. The metagenomics of soil. Nat. Rev. Microbiol. 3, 470–478 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2306">39</a></sup>. To investigate the effects of whole soil microbial communities on the growth of <i>M. micrantha</i> and its neighboring species, we collected soil DNA samples for metagenomic research. With all the soil samples, a total of 25,597,567 nonredundant genes were recovered, with an average open reading frame length of 537 bp. The rarefaction analysis of all soil samples showed a curve approaching saturation (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">29</a>), suggesting that the vast majority of microbial genes in the soil where the three species grew were present in our gene catalog. Genes related to carbohydrate metabolism, energy and nitrogen metabolism, membrane transport, replication, and repair and cell motility in <i>M. micrantha</i> root microbiome was significantly enriched compared to that in its two neighbors and the control (<i>p</i> &lt; 0.05, Duncan-test; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig6">6b</a> and Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">30</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">31</a>), indicating that the root microbiome of <i>M. micrantha</i> was more active than that of the two neighboring species.</p><p>In natural soils, the vast majority of N, P, and K atoms are minimally bioavailable for plants growth, but can be metabolized by various soil microbes, which means that the mineralization processes mediated by soil microbes promoted nutrient cycling in natural ecosystems<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Rovira A. D. Plant root exudates and their influence upon soil microorganisms. (eds K. F. Baker and W. C. Snyder) 170–186. (University of California Press, Berkeley, Los Angeles, 1965)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2338">40</a></sup>. In this study, the relative abundance of key genes involved in nitrogen fixation (<i>nif</i>), assimilatory nitrogen reduction (<i>nirB</i> and <i>nasA</i>), dissimilatory nitrogen reduction (<i>napA</i> and nirB/D), and ammonification (<i>ureC</i>) in <i>M. micrantha</i> soil was higher than that in the soil of its two neighboring native species (<i>P. chinense</i> and <i>P. scandens</i>) soil and the control (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig6">6c, d</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">32</a>), indicating that as a result of the soil microbe functioning, more available forms of nitrogen were reduced to ammonia nitrogen and ammonium was produced and accumulated so as to speed up the nitrogen cycle in <i>M. micrantha</i> soil. In contrast, the relative abundance of key genes involved in denitrification (<i>norB</i>) in <i>M. micrantha</i> soil was significantly lower than that in <i>P. chinense</i> and <i>P. scandens</i> soil (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig6">6c, d</a>), suggesting that there were fewer denitrifying bacteria which use nitrates in <i>M. micrantha</i> soil to carry out respiration so as to decrease the loss of available nitrate nitrogen. In addition, the population density of cultured Azotobacter, ammoniated bacteria, and phosphorus- and potassium-solubilizing bacteria (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">33</a>) in <i>M. micrantha</i> soil were significantly higher than that in the soil of its two neighboring species soil.</p><p>Plant metabolites play an important role in plant survival in a given environment and establish ecological relationships between plants and other organisms. To investigate the metabolites effects of <i>M. micrantha</i> on soil nutrient cycling, we added five STLs (anhydroscandenolide, deoxymikanolide, dihydromikanolide, scandenolide, and 3-epi-dihydroscandenolide) separately to the bare soil near an <i>M. micrantha</i> monoculture to investigate the metabolite-microbe interactions. The STLs significantly increased not only the concentration of carbon dioxide (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">34</a>), but also the relative abundance of nitrogen fixation bacteria (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">35</a>), indicating that the activity of microorganisms was improved. Furthermore, the available nutrients, e.g., ammonium nitrogen, were mineralized efficiently in the soil in five STLs treatments, which was significantly higher than that in the control (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">11</a>). These evidences indicated that the STLs of soil may play an important role in enriching microorganisms, which in turn accelerating the nutrient cycling (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#Fig6">6d</a>). On the other hand, root zone is the primary site for interactions between microbes and plants. They provide a very attractive, nutrient-rich niche that harbors large numbers of active microbes that are important for plant health and nutrient uptake<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Alqueres, S. et al. The bacterial superoxide dismutase and glutathione reductase are crucial for endophytic colonization of rice roots by Gluconacetobacter diazotrophicus PAL5. Mol. Plant Microbe Interact. 26, 937–945 (2013)." href="#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2424">41</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Reinhold-Hurek, B., Bunger, W., Burbano, C. S., Sabale, M. &amp; Hurek, T. Roots shaping their microbiome: global hotspots for microbial activity. Annu Rev. Phytopathol. 53, 403–424 (2015)." href="#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2424_1">42</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Velmourougane, K., Prasanna, R. &amp; Saxena, A. K. Agriculturally important microbial biofilms: present status and future prospects. J. Basic Microbiol. 57, 548–573 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2427">43</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p>Given the common name “mile-a-minute”, one of the most distinctive features of <i>M. micrantha</i> is its fast growth, which strongly relies on its photosynthesis ability<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Day, M. et al. Biology and impacts of Pacific islands invasive species. 13. Mikania micrantha Kunth (Asteraceae). Pac. Sci. 70, 257–285 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2443">1</a></sup>. In this study, we found that the variation pattern of malic acid content, citric acid content, stomatal conductance and key genes expression, such as PEPC, CA, NADP-ME, are consistent with classic CAM plants. This result indicates that CO<sub>2</sub> may be absorbed at night by photosynthesis pathways which was similar to CAM and stored in vacuoles as organic acids to supplement the carbon fixation capacity during the day, helping to absorb more carbon dioxide at night in <i>M. micrantha</i>. The CAM pathway is mostly found in dry-desert plants and is rare in normal plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Ming, R. et al. The pineapple genome and the evolution of CAM photosynthesis. Nat. Genet. 47, 1435 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2452">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Osmond, C. B. Crassulacean acid metabolism: a curiosity in context. Annu. Rev. Plant Physiol. 29, 379–414 (1978)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2455">44</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Borland, A. M. et al. Engineering crassulacean acid metabolism to improve water-use efficiency. Trends Plant Sci. 19, 327–338 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2458">45</a></sup>, which was contrast with tropical plant <i>M. micrantha</i>. In addition, the <i>M. micrantha</i> leaves are not succulent, which was a morphological prerequisite for vacuolar storage capacity of organic acids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Cushman, J. C. &amp; Bohnert, H. J. Crassulacean acid metabolism: molecular genetics. Annu Rev. Plant Physiol. Plant Mol. Biol. 50, 305–332 (1999)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2469">46</a></sup>. These evidences suggested that <i>M. micrantha</i> may develop a special mechanism allowing it to achieve a higher photosynthetic rate that enables fast growth, and the details of the photosynthesis mechanism needs further research. Moreover, the stem of <i>M. micrantha</i> also has a high photosynthetic capacity, further enhancing the growth ability of stems. Thus, the special photosynthesis pathways, together with the greater photosynthetic ability of the stem, is thought to be one of the most important factors contributing to the fast growth of this species. Previous studies reported that photosynthetic efficiency could increase by modification of the photosynthetic pathway, such as introducing an intact maize gene for one of the key enzymes in C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis into rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Ku, M. S. et al. High-level expression of maize phosphoenolpyruvate carboxylase in transgenic rice plants. Nat. Biotechnol. 17, 76–80 (1999)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2482">47</a></sup> and the alternative photorespiratory pathways<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="South, P. F., Cavanagh, A. P., Liu, H. W. &amp; Ort, D. R. Synthetic glycolate metabolism pathways stimulate crop growth and productivity in the field. Science 363, 45 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2486">48</a></sup>, implying a photosynthetic pathway by similar CAM at night in C<sub>3</sub> crop could be used to improve their carbon fixation capacity through a modified photosynthetic pathway in the future.</p><p>The well-known allelochemicals STLs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Huang, H. J., Ye, W. H., Wei, X. Y. &amp; Zhang, C. X. Allelopathic potential of sesquiterpene lactones and phenolic constituents from Mikania micrantha H. B. K. Biochem Syst. Ecol. 36, 867–871 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2495">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Ikezawa, N. et al. Lettuce costunolide synthase (CYP71BL2) and its homolog (CYP71BL1) from sunflower catalyze distinct regio- and stereoselective hydroxylations in sesquiterpene lactone metabolism. J. Biol. Chem. 286, 21601–21611 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2498">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Frey, M., Schmauder, K., Pateraki, I. &amp; Spring, O. Biosyinthesis of Eupatolide—a metabolic route for sesquiterpene lactone formation involving the P450 enzyme CYP71DD6. Acs Chem. Biol. 13, 1536–1543 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2501">36</a></sup> have a certain allelopathic effect on associated plants and soil microbes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Callaway, R. M. &amp; Aschehoug, E. T. Invasive plants versus their new and old neighbors: a mechanism for exotic invasion. Science 290, 521–523 (2000)." href="#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2505">49</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Rial, C., Novaes, P., Varela, R. M., Molinillo, J. M. &amp; Macias, F. A. Phytotoxicity of cardoon (Cynara cardunculus) allelochemicals on standard target species and weeds. J. Agric Food Chem. 62, 6699–6706 (2014)." href="#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2505_1">50</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Molinaro, F. et al. The effect of isabelin, a sesquiterpene lactone from Ambrosia artemisiifolia on soil microorganisms and human pathogens. FEMS Microbiol. Lett. 365, fny001 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2508">51</a></sup>. We found that STLs mainly accumulated in the leaves and flowers and were then released into the surrounding soil by litter fall. The STLs in the soil increased the ammonium nitrogen content by regulating microbial activity. Our results showed that the <i>M. micrantha</i> STLs could not only promote nitrogen fixation, assimilatory nitrogen reduction, dissimilatory nitrate reduction to ammonium, and ammonification, but also inhibited denitrification in the soil, indicating that the soil microbes of <i>M. micrantha</i> promoted nitrogen mineralization and decreased the loss of available nitrogen, thereby accelerating nitrogen cycling, which allowed <i>M. micrantha</i> to utilize more nutrients as well as grow rapidly. In addition, we also proposed a putative biosynthesis pathway of STLs, which may be valuable for producing biological herbicides and pharmacological chemicals derived from STLs, benefiting environmental biosecurity and human health.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Plant materials and sequencing</h3><p>Genomic DNA was extracted from a <i>M. micrantha</i> sample, which was collected form Neilingding Island, Shenzhen, China, for constructing four Illumina PCR-free libraries with 350-bp insert size and sequenced on Illumina HiSeq x ten platforms. Totally, 339 and 14 SMRT cells with 20-kb insert library were constructed and sequenced on RSII and Sequel, respectively. Total RNA was extracted from four tissues (root, stem, leaf, and flower, three biological repeats) using the RNA extraction kit (Huayueyang, ZH120, China). The mRNA from all samples was purified using the Truseq RNA Sample Prep Kit (illumina), followed to be sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq 2500 sequencer.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Genome assembly and annotation</h3><p>The Illumina raw reads were filtered by trimming the adapter sequence and low-quality part by in-house software clean_adapter and clean_lowqual (<a href="https://github.com/fanagislab/assembly_2ndGeneration/tree/master/clean_illumina">https://github.com/fanagislab/assembly_2ndGeneration/tree/master/clean_illumina</a>), resulting in a clean and high-quality reads data with average error rate &lt;0.001. The raw PacBio reads were assembled by the software canu-1.6<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Berlin, K. et al. Assembling large genomes with single-molecule sequencing and locality-sensitive hashing. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 623–630 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2548">52</a></sup>, and contaminating heterozygous fragments were filtered by using the result of falcon/falcon-unzip<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Chin, C. S. et al. Phased diploid genome assembly with single-molecule real-time sequencing. Nat. Methods 13, 1050–1054 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2552">53</a></sup>. We used the alternative heterozygous haplotype sequence (hapotig) assembled by falcon/falcon-unzip to align to the contigs assembled by canu and d filtered out the heterozygous contigs from canu assembled result (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">1</a>). Then Illumina reads were aligned to the contigs by BWA-MEM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754–1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2559">54</a></sup>, and single base errors in the contigs were corrected by Pilon-v1.22<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS One 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2564">55</a></sup>. For the Hi-C-based proximity-guided assembly, we removed duplications and kept reads that uniquely mapped to the reference genome. The assembly package, Lachesis was applied to do clustering, ordering and orienting<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Burton, J. N. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interactions. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1119–1125 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2568">56</a></sup>.</p><p>The gene models were predicted by EVidence Modeler v1.1.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the Program to Assemble Spliced Alignments. Genome Biol. 9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2575">57</a></sup>, embedded in a pipeline that integrating evidences from <i>ab initio</i> predictions, homology-based searches, and full-length transcriptome and RNA-seq alignments. Then, the protein-coding sequences were mapped by RNA-seq data and functionally annotated using UniProt<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Wu, C. H. et al. The Universal Protein Resource (UniProt): an expanding universe of protein information. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, D187–D191 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2582">58</a></sup> and KEGG databases<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Kanehisa, M., Goto, S., Kawashima, S., Okuno, Y. &amp; Hattori, M. The KEGG resource for deciphering the genome. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, D277–D280 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2586">59</a></sup>. Finally, the gene models were retained if they had at least one supporting evidence. Gene functional annotation was performed by aligning the protein sequences to UniProt, eggNOG and KEGG databases using BLASTP v2.3.0+ (<a href="http://blast.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/Blast.cgi">http://blast.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/Blast.cgi</a>) with E-value cutoff of 10<sup>−5</sup>. The pathway analysis and functional classification were conducted based on KEGG database. InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Quevillon, E. et al. InterProScan: protein domains identifier. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, W116–W120 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2597">60</a></sup> was used to assign preliminary GO terms, Pfam domains and IPR domains to the gene models.</p><p>A de novo repeat library for <i>M. micrantha</i> was constructed by RepeatModeler (v1.0.4; <a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler/</a>). TEs were identified by RepeatMasker (v4.0.6; <a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>) using both Repbase library and the de novo library. Tandem repeats were predicted using Tandem Repeats Finder v4.07b<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573–580 (1999)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2615">61</a></sup>. The LTR-RTs were annotated with an in-house pipeline that uses LTR-finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265–W268 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2619">62</a></sup> and LTR-retriever<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Ou, S. &amp; Jiang, N. LTR_retriever: A highly accurate and sensitive program for identification of long terminal repeat retrotransposons. Plant Physiol. 176, 1410–1422 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2624">63</a></sup>. The LTR insert time is estimated by LTR-retriever.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Evolution analysis</h3><p>Duplicated genomic fragments were identified by MCscanX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Tang, H. et al. Synteny and collinearity in plant genomes. Science 320, 486–488 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2636">64</a></sup>, requiring at least ten paralogous gene pairs per collinear block, and the duplicate_gene_classifier in MCscanX was implemented to classify the origins of the duplicate genes into different types. Orthologous and paralogous gene families were assigned by OrthoFinder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. OrthoFinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy. Genome Biol. 16, 157 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2640">65</a></sup> with default parameters.</p><p>Gene families that contain only one gene for each species were selected to construct the phylogenetic tree. The protein sequences of each gene family were independently aligned by muscle v3.8.31<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792–1797 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2647">66</a></sup> and then concatenated into one super-sequence. The phylogenetic tree was constructed by maximum likelihood (ML) using PhyML v3.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Guindon, S. et al. New algorithms and methods to estimate maximum-likelihood phylogenies: assessing the performance of PhyML 3.0. Syst. Biol. 59, 307–321 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2651">67</a></sup> with best-fit model (MtMAN) that was estimated by ProtTest3<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Darriba, D., Taboada, G. L., Doallo, R. &amp; Posada, D. ProtTest 3: fast selection of best-fit models of protein evolution. Bioinformatics 27, 1164–1165 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2655">68</a></sup>. The Bayesian Relaxed Molecular Clock (BRMC) approach was adopted to estimate the neutral evolutionary rate and species divergence time using the program MCMCTree, implemented in PAML v4.9 package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586–1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2659">69</a></sup>. The calibration time interval (110–124 Mya) of grape was adopted from TimeTree (<a href="http://www.timetree.org">http://www.timetree.org</a>). We used the the inter- and intra-syntentic gene pairs to calculate the synonymous mutation rate (<i>K</i><sub><i>s</i></sub>) values by KaKs_Calculator 2.0 with default parameter<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Wang, D., Zhang, Y., Zhang, Z., Zhu, J. &amp; Yu, J. KaKs_Calculator 2.0: a toolkit incorporating gamma-series methods and sliding window strategies. Genomics Proteom. Bioinforma. 8, 77–80 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2674">70</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Transcriptome data analysis</h3><p>Transcriptome reads were filtered by in-house software clean_adapter and clean_lowqual (<a href="https://github.com/fanagislab/assembly_2ndGeneration/tree/master/clean_illumina">https://github.com/fanagislab/assembly_2ndGeneration/tree/master/clean_illumina</a>) and then mapped to the reference genome of <i>M. micrantha</i> using TopHat v. 2.1.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Kim, D. et al. TopHat2: accurate alignment of transcriptomes in the presence of insertions, deletions and gene fusions. Genome Biol. 14, R36 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2693">71</a></sup> with default settings. Reads count were calculated with HTSeq v. 0.9.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Anders, S., Pyl, P. T. &amp; Huber, W. HTSeq—a Python framework to work with high-throughput sequencing data. Bioinformatics 31, 166–169 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2697">72</a></sup> using BAM results from TopHat v. 2.1.0, and TPM values were then calculated for every gene in the samples. We continue to identify the differentially expressed genes by DEseq2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Love, M. I., Huber, W. &amp; Anders, S. Moderated estimation of fold change and dispersion for RNA-seq data with DESeq2. Genome Biol. 15, 550 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2701">73</a></sup>. Co-expression network analysis was performed by WGCNA package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Hollender, C. A. et al. Floral transcriptomes in woodland strawberry uncover developing receptacle and anther gene networks. Plant Physiol. 165, 1062–1075 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d15635e2706">74</a></sup> using all transcriptomes. WGCNA network construction and module detection was identified using an unsigned type of topological overlap matrix with a soft-thresholding power <i>β</i> of 28 (correlation coefficient <i>R</i><sup>2</sup> &gt; 0.9).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">The STLs measure</h3><p>Five STLs were isolated from the aerial part of <i>M. micrantha</i> by repeated chromatography. The structures of STLs were elucidated by nuclear magnetic resonance and high resolution electrospray ionization MS. UPLC coupled to MS was used as a method for quantitative analysis of five STLs (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">12</a>) and the chemical constituents of different plant tissues (for details see the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">Supplementary Information</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Statistical analysis</h3><p>All statistical tests were performed using SPSS 11.5 software (SPSS Inc., USA). Soil chemical characteristics, nitrogen, phosphorus, potassium content of plant tissue and the population density of soil cultured bacteria were, respectively, compared using one-way ANOVA, followed by least significant difference tests at <i>p</i> &lt; 0.05 or Duncan’s multiple range test, <i>p</i> &lt; 0.05. All observations are independent of one another and scores in groups are normally distributed. A univariate <i>F</i>-test for each variable was used to interpret the respective effects. The equality of error variances was tested by using Levene’s test and the error variance of the dependent variable was considered to be equal across groups when <i>p</i> &gt; 0.05. In the soil metagenomic analysis, the significant functional differences between <i>M. micrantha</i> and two neighboring species, control samples were determined by the Duncan-test.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Reporting summary</h3><p>Further information on research design is available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM3">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>Data supporting the findings of this work are available within the paper and its <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">Supplementary Information</a> files. A reporting summary for this Article is available as a <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">Supplementary Information</a> file. The datasets generated and analyzed during the current study are available from the corresponding author upon request. The Whole Genome Shotgun project of <i>Mikaina micrantha</i> has been deposited at DDBJ/ENA/GenBank under the accession SZYD00000000 [<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/CM018680.1/">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/CM018680.1/</a>] with BioProject ID <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA528368">PRJNA528368</a>. The version described in this paper is version SZYD01000000. For details, PacBio SMRT data are under accessions <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/SRR8816384">SRR8816384</a> and <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/SRR8834228">SRR8834228</a>–<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/SRR8834566">SRR8834566</a>; genomic Illumina DNA data are under accessions <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/SRR8835135">SRR8835135</a>, <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/SRR8835136">SRR8835136</a>, and <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/SRR8835137">SRR8835137</a>; RNA-seq data are under accessions <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/SRR8857616">SRR8857616</a>–<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/SRR8857640">SRR8857640</a>; full-length transcriptome data are under accessions <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/SRR8834701">SRR8834701</a>–<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/SRR8834748">SRR8834748</a>. For the defoliation experiment, the RNA-seq data have been deposited as <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/SRR8846782">SRR8846782</a>–<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/SRR8846787">SRR8846787</a>. The metagenomics data have been deposited as <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/SRR8936416">SRR8936416</a>–<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/SRR8936475">SRR8936475</a>. The <i>M. micrantha</i> genome assembly, gene prediction and functional annotation datas in this paper can also be accessed at ftp://ftp.agis.org.cn/Mikania_micrantha/. The source data underlying Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM4">3</a>b–d, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM4">4</a>a, b, e, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM4">5</a>b, d, e, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM4">6a–c</a>, as well as Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">13</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">16</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">17</a>a, b, d, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">18</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">20</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">22</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">26</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-13926-4#MOESM1">10</a> and <a data-track="click"             </div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="code-availability" data-title="Code availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="code-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="code-availability">Code availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="code-availability-content">
              
              <p>The in-house software clean_adapter [<a href="https://github.com/fanagislab/assembly_2ndGeneration/blob/master/clean_illumina/clean_adapter">https://github.com/fanagislab/assembly_2ndGeneration/blob/master/clean_illumina/clean_adapter</a>] and clean_lowqual [<a href="https://github.com/fanagislab/assembly_2ndGeneration/blob/master/clean_illumina/clean_lowqual">https://github.com/fanagislab/assembly_2ndGeneration/blob/master/clean_illumina/clean_lowqual</a>] are used to filter the adapter and low-quality sequence.</p>
            </div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="change-history" data-title="Change history"><div class="c-article-section" id="change-history-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="change-history">Change history</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="change-history-content"><ul class="c-article-change-list"><li class="c-article-change-list__item" id="chg1"><ins datetime="2020-04-27"><h3 class="c-article-change-list__heading u-h3">27 April 2020</h3><div class="c-article-change-list__details"><p>In the original version of this Article, the surname of reviewer Hélène Badouin was incorrectly spelled as ‘Badoui’. In addition, the surname of reviewer Robert VanBuren was incorrectly spelled as ‘VanBure’. The ‘Peer review information’ text should read ‘Nature Communications thanks Hélène Badouin, Maximilian Frey and Robert VanBuren for their contribution to the peer review of this work. Peer reviewer reports are available.’ These have been corrected in both the PDF and HTML versions of the Article.</p></div></ins></li></ul></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Bib1" data-title="References"><div class="c-article-section" id="Bib1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Bib1">References</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Bib1-content"><div data-container-section="references"><ol class="c-article-references"><li class="c-article-references__item js-c-reading-companion-references-item" itemprop="citation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle"><meta itemprop="author" content="M.
